<p><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Iraqi_Kurdistan&amp;params=36_55_N_44_2_E_source:plwiki_region:IQ_scale:10000"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">36°55′N</span> <span class="longitude">44°2′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">&#xfeff; / &#xfeff;</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">36.917°N 44.033°E</span><span style="display:none">&#xfeff; / <span class="geo">36.917; 44.033</span></span></span></a></span></span></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="History of the Kurdish people">History of the Kurdish people</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Islamic_period">Pre-Islamic period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jarmo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jarmo.jpg/220px-Jarmo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jarmo.jpg/330px-Jarmo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Jarmo.jpg/440px-Jarmo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="862" data-file-height="774" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jarmo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Neolithic village of <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">Jarmo</a></div></div></div>
<p>In prehistoric times, the region was home to a <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> culture such as has been found at the <a href="/wiki/Shanidar_Cave" title="Shanidar Cave">Shanidar Cave</a>. The region was host to the <a href="/wiki/Jarmo" title="Jarmo">Jarmo</a> culture <a href="/wiki/Circa" title="Circa">circa</a> 7000&#160;BC. The earliest <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">neolithic</a> site in Kurdistan is at <a href="/wiki/Hassuna" class="mw-redirect" title="Hassuna">Tell Hassuna</a>, the centre of the <i>Hassuna culture</i>, circa 6000&#160;BC. The region was inhabited by the northern branch of the <a href="/wiki/Gutian_people" title="Gutian people">Gutian</a>/<a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">Hurrians</a> around 2400 BC.
</p><p>It was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> from 2334&#160;BC until 2154&#160;BC. <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> kings are attested from the 23rd century BC according to the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_King_List" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian King List">Assyrian King List</a>, and Assyrian <a href="/wiki/City-states" class="mw-redirect" title="City-states">city-states</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ekallatum" title="Ekallatum">Ekallatum</a> started appearing in the region from the mid-21st century BC. Prior to the rule of king <a href="/wiki/Ushpia" title="Ushpia">Ushpia</a> circa 2030&#160;BC, the city of Ashur appears to have been a regional administrative center of the Akkadian Empire, implicated by <a href="/wiki/Nuzi#Archaeology" title="Nuzi">Nuzi tablets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Shendge1997_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shendge1997-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> subject to their fellow Akkadian <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon</a> and his successors.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Large cities were built by the Assyrians, including <a href="/wiki/Ashur" title="Ashur">Ashur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nineveh" title="Nineveh">Nineveh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guzana" class="mw-redirect" title="Guzana">Guzana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arrapkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Arrapkha">Arrapkha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imgur-Enlil" class="mw-redirect" title="Imgur-Enlil">Imgur-Enlil</a> (<a href="/wiki/Balawat" title="Balawat">Balawat</a>), <a href="/wiki/Shubat-Enlil" class="mw-redirect" title="Shubat-Enlil">Shubat-Enlil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalhu">Kalhu</a> (<a href="/wiki/Calah" class="mw-redirect" title="Calah">Calah</a>&#160;/&#32;<a href="/wiki/Nimrud" title="Nimrud">Nimrud</a>). One of the major Assyrian cities in the area, <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a> (Arba-Ilu), was noted for its distinctive cult of <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-semorig_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-semorig-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> and the city was called "the Lady of Ishtar" by its Assyrian inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The Assyrians ruled the region from the 21st century BC.
</p><p>The region was known as Assyria, and was the center of various Assyrian empires (particularly during the periods 1813–1754&#160;BC, 1385–1076&#160;BC and the <a href="/wiki/Neo_Assyrian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo Assyrian Empire">Neo Assyrian Empire</a> of 911–608&#160;BC. Between 612 and 605&#160;BC, the Assyrian empire fell and it passed to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Babylonian_Empire" title="Neo-Babylonian Empire">neo-Babylonians</a> and later became part of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Athura Satrap</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Achaemenian Empire">Achaemenian Empire</a> from 539 to 332&#160;BC, where it was known as <a href="/wiki/Athura" class="mw-redirect" title="Athura">Athura</a>, the Achaemenid name for Assyria.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The region fell to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_The_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander The Great">Alexander The Great</a> in 332&#160;BC and was thereafter ruled by the Greek <a href="/wiki/Seleucid" class="mw-redirect" title="Seleucid">Seleucid</a> Empire until the mid 2nd century BC (and was renamed <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, a Greek corruption of Assyria), when it fell to <a href="/wiki/Mithridates_I_of_Parthia" title="Mithridates I of Parthia">Mithridates I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> semi-independent kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a> was centred in Erbil in the first <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Chronicle_of_Arbela_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chronicle_of_Arbela-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Later, the region was incorporated by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Assyria" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Assyria">Roman Assyria</a> province but shortly retaken by the <a href="/wiki/Sassanids" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanids">Sassanids</a> who established the <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">Satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Assuristan" class="mw-redirect" title="Assuristan">Assuristan</a> (Sassanid Assyria) in it until the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Iran">Arab Islamic conquest</a>. The region became a center of the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Church_of_the_East" title="Assyrian Church of the East">Assyrian Church of the East</a> and a flourishing Syriac literary tradition during Sassanid rule.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_period">Islamic period</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png/220px-Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png/330px-Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png/440px-Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png 2x" data-file-width="549" data-file-height="743" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ottoman_vilayets_of_Van_and_Mossoul_1899.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Vilayets" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilayets">vilayets</a> of Van and Mossoul, 1899. Modern Iraqi Kurdistan is covered by the Mosul vilayet (green), which is divided into the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak" title="Sanjak">sanjaks</a> of Mossoul (<a href="/wiki/Nineveh_Governorate" title="Nineveh Governorate">Mosul</a>), Kerkouk (<a href="/wiki/Kirkuk_Governorate" title="Kirkuk Governorate">Kirkuk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erbil_Governorate" title="Erbil Governorate">Erbil</a>), and Souleimanié (<a href="/wiki/Sulaymaniyah_Governorate" title="Sulaymaniyah Governorate">Sulaymaniyah</a>). To the east is Persia and south is the vilayet of Bagdad.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_(1922-1924)_(Mosul_vilayet).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg/220px-Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg/330px-Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg/440px-Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_%281922-1924%29_%28Mosul_vilayet%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1851" data-file-height="1443" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethnographical_Map_of_the_contested_territory_(1922-1924)_(Mosul_vilayet).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ethnographical Map of the contested territory, compiled by the Commission according to the latest statistics drawn up by the Government of Iraq (1922-1924), League of Nations. Green shows the kurd polulation in the region, yellow shows arabs, purple yazidis</div></div></div>
<p>The region was conquered by <a href="/wiki/Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> in the mid 7th century AD as the invading forces <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim conquest of Iran">conquered the Sassanian Empire</a>, while Assyria was dissolved as a geo-political entity (although Assyrians remain in the area to this day), and the area made part of the Muslim Arab <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad</a>, and later the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid</a> Caliphates, before becoming part of various <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mongol" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> <a href="/wiki/Emirate" title="Emirate">emirates</a>. Following the disintegration of the <a href="/wiki/Ak_Koyunlu" class="mw-redirect" title="Ak Koyunlu">Ak Koyunlu</a>, all of its territories including what is modern-day Iraqi Kurdistan passed to the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Safavids" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavids">Safavids</a> in the earliest 16th century.
</p><p>Between the 16th and 17th century the area nowadays known as Iraqi Kurdistan, (formerly ruled by three principalities of <a href="/wiki/Baban" title="Baban">Baban</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badinan_Emirate" class="mw-redirect" title="Badinan Emirate">Badinan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soran_Emirate" title="Soran Emirate">Soran</a>) was continuously passed back and forth between archrivals the <a href="/wiki/Safavid_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Safavid Dynasty">Safavids</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottomans</a>, until the Ottomans managed to decisively seize power in the region starting from the mid 17th century through the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Safavid_War_(1623%E2%80%9339)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)">Ottoman–Safavid War (1623–39)</a> and the resulting <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zuhab" title="Treaty of Zuhab">Treaty of Zuhab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Roemer285_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roemer285-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In the early 18th century it briefly passed to the Iranian <a href="/wiki/Afsharids" class="mw-redirect" title="Afsharids">Afsharids</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Nader_Shah" title="Nader Shah">Nader Shah</a>. Following Nader's death in 1747, Ottoman suzerainty was reimposed, and in 1831, direct <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> rule was established which lasted until <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, when the Ottomans were defeated by the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kurdish_states_1835.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Kurdish_states_1835.png/220px-Kurdish_states_1835.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Kurdish_states_1835.png/330px-Kurdish_states_1835.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Kurdish_states_1835.png/440px-Kurdish_states_1835.png 2x" data-file-width="1566" data-file-height="1569" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kurdish_states_1835.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kurdish Independent Kingdoms and Autonomous Principalities circa 1835</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kurdish_revolts_under_British_control">Kurdish revolts under British control</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahmud_Barzanji.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Mahmud_Barzanji.jpg/220px-Mahmud_Barzanji.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="357" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/Mahmud_Barzanji.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="308" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mahmud_Barzanji.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Barzanji" title="Mahmud Barzanji">Mahmud Barzanji</a>, leader of a series of Kurdish uprisings against the British Mandate of Iraq</div></div></div>
<p>During World War I, the British and French divided <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sykes-Picot_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Sykes-Picot Agreement">Sykes-Picot Agreement</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of Sèvres">Treaty of Sèvres</a> (which did not enter into force), and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a> which superseded it, led to the advent of modern Western Asia and the modern Republic of Turkey. The <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> granted France mandates over <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Syria" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Mandate_of_Lebanon" class="mw-redirect" title="French Mandate of Lebanon">Lebanon</a> and granted the United Kingdom mandates over <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Palestine</a> (which then consisted of two autonomous regions: <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Palestine" title="Mandatory Palestine">Mandatory Palestine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Transjordan" title="Emirate of Transjordan">Transjordan</a>) and what was to become <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iraq_(British_administration)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Iraq (British administration)">Iraq</a>. Parts of the Ottoman Empire on the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> were eventually taken over by <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_(1923).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png/250px-Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png/375px-Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png/500px-Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_%281923%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1106" data-file-height="996" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kingdom_of_Kurdistan_(1923).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kingdom of Kurdistan in 1923</div></div></div>
<p>On December 1, 1918, during a meeting in <a href="/wiki/Sulaymaniyah" title="Sulaymaniyah">Sulaymaniyah</a> with Colonel <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Wilson" title="Arnold Wilson">Arnold Wilson</a>, the Acting Civil Commissioner for Mesopotamia, Kurdish leaders called for British support for a united and independent Kurdistan under British protection.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Between 1919 and 1922, Shaikh <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Barzanji" title="Mahmud Barzanji">Mahmud Barzanji</a>, an influential Kurdish leader based in Sulaymaniyah, formed a Kurdish government and led <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Barzanji_revolts" title="Mahmud Barzanji revolts">two revolts</a> against the British rule. It took the British authorities until 1924 to put down his uprisings. The first revolt began on May 22, 1919 with the arrest of British officials in Sulaymaniyah and it quickly spread to <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> and Erbil. The British employed aerial bombardments, artillery, and ground attacks by <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Indian" title="Anglo-Indian">Anglo-Indian</a> troops and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Levies" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrian Levies">Assyrian Levies</a>, to quell the uprising.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2017)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>With the collapse of the Kurdish forces, the British exiled Mahmud Barzanji to India. In July 1920, 62 tribal leaders of the region called for the independence of Kurdistan under a British mandate. The objection of the British to Kurdish self-rule sprang from the fear that success of an independent Kurdish area would tempt the two <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arab</a> areas of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basra" title="Basra">Basra</a> to follow suit, hence endangering the direct British control over all Mesopotamia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1922, Britain restored Shaikh Mahmud to power, hoping that he would organize the Kurds to act as a buffer against the <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turks</a>, who had territorial claims over <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a>. However, defiant to the British, in 1922 Shaikh Mahmoud declared a <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kurdistan" title="Kingdom of Kurdistan">Kurdish Kingdom</a> with himself as king. It took two years for the British to bring Kurdish areas into submission, while Shaikh Mahmud found refuge in an unknown location.
</p><p>In 1930, following the announcement of the admission of Iraq to the League of Nations, Shaikh Mahmoud started a third uprising which was suppressed with British air and ground forces.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1927, the <a href="/wiki/Barzani_Kurds" title="Barzani Kurds">Barzani clan</a> had become vocal supporters of Kurdish rights in Iraq. In 1929, the Barzani demanded the formation of a Kurdish province in northern Iraq. Emboldened by these demands, in 1931 Kurdish notables petitioned the League of Nations to set up an independent Kurdish government. In late 1931, <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Barzani" title="Ahmed Barzani">Ahmed Barzani</a> initiated a Kurdish <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_Barzani_revolt" title="Ahmed Barzani revolt">rebellion</a> against Iraq, and though defeated within several months, the movement gained a major importance in the Kurdish struggle later on, creating the ground for such a notable Kurdish rebel as <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Barzani" title="Mustafa Barzani">Mustafa Barzani</a>.
</p><p>During World War II, the power vacuum in Iraq was exploited by the Kurdish tribes and under the leadership of Mustafa Barzani a rebellion broke out in the north, effectively gaining control of Kurdish areas until 1945, when Iraqis could once again subdue the Kurds with British support. Under pressure from the Iraqi government and the British, the most influential leader of the clan, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Barzani" title="Mustafa Barzani">Mustafa Barzani</a> was forced into exile in Iran in 1945. Later he moved to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Mahabad" title="Republic of Mahabad">Republic of Mahabad</a> in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Barzani_Revolt_.281960.E2.80.931970.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Barzani_Revolt_(1960–1970)">Barzani Revolt (1960–1970)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_War" title="First Iraqi–Kurdish War">First Iraqi–Kurdish War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg/220px-3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg/330px-3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg/440px-3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:3.7inchHowitzerBarzanOperations1932.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_War" title="First Iraqi–Kurdish War">The Barzani revolt</a>, June 1932</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/14_July_Revolution" title="14 July Revolution">military coup</a> by <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">Abdul Karim Qasim</a> in 1958, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Barzani" title="Mustafa Barzani">Mustafa Barzani</a> was invited by Qasim to return from exile, where he was greeted with a hero's welcome. As part of the deal arranged between Qasim and Barzani, Qasim had promised to give the Kurds regional autonomy in return for Barzani's support for his policies.  Meanwhile, during 1959–1960, Barzani became the head of the <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Democratic_Party" title="Kurdistan Democratic Party">Kurdistan Democratic Party</a> (KDP), which was granted legal status in 1960. By early 1960, it became apparent that Qasim would not follow through with his promise of regional autonomy.  As a result, the KDP began to agitate for regional autonomy. In the face of growing Kurdish dissent, as well as Barzani's personal power, Qasim began to incite the Barzanis historical enemies, the <i>Baradost</i> and <i>Zebari</i> tribes, which led to intertribal warfare throughout 1960 and early 1961.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg/220px-Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg/330px-Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg 2x" data-file-width="340" data-file-height="213" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barzani_%26_Qasim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Barzani" title="Mustafa Barzani">Mustafa Barzani</a> with <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Karim_Qasim" title="Abd al-Karim Qasim">Abd al-Karim Qasim</a></div></div></div>
<p>By February 1961, Barzani had successfully defeated the pro-government forces and consolidated his position as leader of the Kurds. At this point, Barzani ordered his forces to occupy and expel government officials from all Kurdish territory. This was not received well in Baghdad, and as a result, Qasim began to prepare for a military offensive against the north to return government control of the region. Meanwhile, in June 1961, the KDP issued a detailed ultimatum to Qasim outlining Kurdish grievances and demanded rectification. Qasim ignored the Kurdish demands and continued his planning for war.
</p><p>It was not until September 10, when an Iraqi army column was ambushed by a group of Kurds, that the Kurdish revolt truly began. In response to the attack, Qasim lashed out and ordered the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Air_Force" title="Iraqi Air Force">Iraqi Air Force</a> to indiscriminately bomb Kurdish villages, which ultimately served to rally the entire Kurdish population to Barzani's standard. Due to Qasim's profound distrust of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a>, which he purposely failed to adequately arm (in fact, Qasim implemented a policy of ammunition rationing), Qasim's government was not able to subdue the insurrection. This stalemate irritated powerful factions within the military and is said to be one of the main reasons behind the <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athism" title="Ba&#39;athism">Ba'athist</a> coup against Qasim in February 1963. In November 1963, after considerable infighting amongst the civilian and military wings of the Ba'athists, they were ousted by <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Salam_Arif" title="Abdul Salam Arif">Abdul Salam Arif</a> in a coup. Then, after another failed offensive, Arif declared a ceasefire in February 1964 which provoked a split among Kurdish urban radicals on one hand and <a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Peshmerga</a> (Freedom fighters) forces led by Barzani on the other.
</p><p>Barzani agreed to the ceasefire and fired the radicals from the party. Following the unexpected death of Arif, whereupon he was replaced by his brother, <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Rahman_Arif" title="Abdul Rahman Arif">Abdul Rahman Arif</a>, the Iraqi government launched a last-ditch effort to defeat the Kurds. This campaign failed in May 1966, when Barzani forces thoroughly defeated the Iraqi Army at the Battle of Mount Handrin, near <a href="/wiki/Rawandiz" title="Rawandiz">Rawandiz</a>. At this battle, it was said that the Kurds slaughtered an entire brigade.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Recognizing the futility of continuing this campaign, Rahamn Arif announced a 12-point peace program in June 1966, which was not implemented due to the overthrow of Rahman Arif in a 1968 coup by the <a href="/wiki/Baath_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Baath Party">Baath Party</a>.
</p><p>The Ba'ath government started a campaign to end the Kurdish insurrection, which stalled in 1969. This can be partly attributed to the internal power struggle in Baghdad and also tensions with Iran. Moreover, the Soviet Union  pressured the Iraqis to come to terms with Barzani. A peace plan was announced in March 1970 and provided for broader Kurdish autonomy. The plan also gave Kurds representation in government bodies, to be implemented in four years.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>  Despite this, the Iraqi government embarked on an Arabization program in the oil rich regions of Kirkuk and <a href="/wiki/Khanaqin" title="Khanaqin">Khanaqin</a> in the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the following years, Baghdad government overcame its internal divisions and concluded a treaty of friendship with the Soviet Union in April 1972 and ended its isolation within the Arab world. On the other hand, Kurds remained dependent on the Iranian military support and could do little to strengthen their forces.
</p>
<h3><span id="Autonomy_negotiations_.281970.E2.80.931974.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomy_negotiations_(1970–1974)">Autonomy negotiations (1970–1974)</span></h3>
<p>Regional autonomy had originally been established in 1970 with the creation of the <i>Kurdish Autonomous Region</i> following the agreement of an Autonomy Accord between the government of Iraq and leaders of the Iraqi Kurdish community. A Legislative Assembly was established and Erbil became the capital of the new entity which lay in Northern Iraq, encompassing the Kurdish authorities of Erbil, Dahuk and Sulaymaniyah. The one-party rule which had dominated Iraq however meant that the new assembly was an overall component of Baghdad's central government; the Kurdish authority was installed by Baghdad and no multi-party system had been inaugurated in Iraqi Kurdistan, and as such the local population enjoyed no particular democratic freedom denied to the rest of the country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Kurdish_Iraqi_War_Algiers_Agreement">Second Kurdish Iraqi War  Algiers Agreement</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Second_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_War" title="Second Iraqi–Kurdish War">Second Iraqi–Kurdish War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:183px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kurdistan_1975.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kurdistan_1975.png/181px-Kurdistan_1975.png" decoding="async" width="181" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kurdistan_1975.png/272px-Kurdistan_1975.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kurdistan_1975.png/362px-Kurdistan_1975.png 2x" data-file-width="1106" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kurdistan_1975.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kurdistan Autonomous Region in 1975</div></div></div>
<p>In 1973, the US made a secret agreement with the Shah of Iran to begin covertly funding Kurdish rebels against Bagdad through the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> and in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Mossad" title="Mossad">Mossad</a>, both of which would be active in the country through the launch of the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Iraq">Iraqi invasion</a> and into the present.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> By 1974, the Iraqi government retaliated with a <a href="/wiki/Second_Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_War" title="Second Iraqi–Kurdish War">new offensive</a> against the Kurds and pushed them close to the border with Iran. Iraq informed <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a> that it was willing to satisfy other Iranian demands in return for an end to its aid to the Kurds. With mediation by <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algerian</a> President <a href="/wiki/Houari_Boumediene" class="mw-redirect" title="Houari Boumediene">Houari Boumediene</a>, Iran and Iraq reached a comprehensive settlement in March 1975 known as the <i><a href="/wiki/1975_Algiers_Agreement" title="1975 Algiers Agreement">Algiers Pact</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> The agreement left the Kurds helpless and Tehran cut supplies to the Kurdish movement. Barzani went to Iran with many of his supporters. Others surrendered <i>en masse</i> and the rebellion ended after a few days.
</p><p>As a result, Iraqi government extended its control over the northern region after 15 years and in order to secure its influence, started an <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabization</a> program by moving Arabs to the vicinity of oil fields in Kurdistan, particularly those around Kirkuk.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The repressive measures carried out by the government against the Kurds after the Algiers agreement led to renewed clashes between the Iraqi Army and Kurdish guerrillas in 1977. In 1978 and 1979, 600 Kurdish villages were burned down and around 200,000 Kurds were deported to the other parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Farouk-Sluglett_24_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farouk-Sluglett_24-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabization_campaign_and_PUK_insurgency">Arabization campaign and PUK insurgency</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/PUK_insurgency" title="PUK insurgency">PUK insurgency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Arabization_campaigns_in_North_Iraq" title="Ba&#39;athist Arabization campaigns in North Iraq">Ba'athist Arabization campaigns in North Iraq</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ba%27ath_Party_(Iraq)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ba&#39;ath Party (Iraq)">Ba'athist</a> government of Iraq <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Arabization_campaigns_in_North_Iraq" title="Ba&#39;athist Arabization campaigns in North Iraq">forcibly displaced and culturally Arabized</a> minorities (<a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Iraq" title="Kurds in Iraq">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yezidis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabak people">Shabaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Iraq" title="Armenians in Iraq">Armenians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmen" title="Iraqi Turkmen">Turkmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandeans">Mandeans</a>), in line with <a href="/wiki/Settler_colonialism" title="Settler colonialism">settler colonialist</a> policies, from the 1960s to the early 2000s, in order to shift the demographics of North Iraq towards Arab domination. The Baath party under <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> engaged in active expulsion of minorities from the mid-1970s onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-savelberg_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-savelberg-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 1978 and 1979, 600 Kurdish villages were burned down and around 200,000 Kurds were deported to the other parts of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Farouk-Sluglett_24_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farouk-Sluglett_24-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The campaigns took place during the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi%E2%80%93Kurdish_conflict" title="Iraqi–Kurdish conflict">Iraqi–Kurdish conflict</a>, being largely motivated by the Kurdish–Arab ethnic and political conflict. Arab settlement programs reached their peak during the late 1970s, in line with depopulation efforts of the Ba'athist regime. The Baathist policies motivating those events are sometimes referred to as "internal colonialism",<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> described by Dr. Francis Kofi Abiew as a "Colonial 'Arabization<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>" program, including large-scale Kurdish deportations and forced Arab settlement in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Iran.E2.80.93Iraq_War_and_Anfal_Campaign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran–Iraq_War_and_Anfal_Campaign">Iran–Iraq War and Anfal Campaign</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_rebellion_of_1983" title="Kurdish rebellion of 1983">Kurdish rebellion of 1983</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg/220px-Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg/330px-Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg/440px-Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_Graves_for_Victims_of_1988_Chemical_Attack_-_Halabja_-_Kurdistan_-_Iraq.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graves of the Halabja chemical attack victims</div></div></div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>, the Iraqi government again implemented anti-Kurdish policies and a <i>de facto</i> civil war broke out. Iraq was widely condemned by the international community, but was never seriously punished for oppressive measures, including the use of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">chemical weapons</a> against the Kurds,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Halabja_chemical_attack" title="Halabja chemical attack">which resulted in thousands of deaths</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/Al-Anfal_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Anfal Campaign">Al-Anfal Campaign</a> constituted  a systematic <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish people</a> in Iraq. The first wave of the plan was carried out in 1982 when 8,000 Barzanis were arrested and their remains were returned to Kurdistan in 2008.
</p><p>The second and more extensive and widespread wave began from March 29, 1987 until April 23, 1989, when the Iraqi army under the command of <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Ali_Hassan_al-Majid" title="Ali Hassan al-Majid">Ali Hassan al-Majid</a> carried out a genocidal campaign against the Kurds, characterized by the following <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> violations: The widespread use of chemical weapons, the wholesale destruction of some 2,000 villages, and slaughter of around 50,000 rural Kurds, by the most conservative estimates. The large Kurdish town of <a href="/wiki/Qala_Dizeh" class="mw-redirect" title="Qala Dizeh">Qala Dizeh</a> (population 70,000) was completely destroyed by the Iraqi army. The campaign also included Arabization of Kirkuk, a program to drive Kurds and other ethnic groups out of the oil-rich city and replace them with Arab settlers from central and southern Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autonomous_period">Autonomous period</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_Persian_Gulf_War">After the Persian Gulf War</span></h4>
<p>Even though autonomy had been agreed in 1970, local population enjoyed no particular democratic freedom denied to the rest of the country. Things began to change after the 1991 uprising against <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a> at the end of the Persian Gulf War. <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_688" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 688">Resolution 688</a> gave birth to a <i>safe haven</i> following international concern for the safety of Kurdish refugees. The U.S. and the Coalition established a No Fly Zone over a large part of northern Iraq (see <a href="/wiki/Operation_Provide_Comfort" title="Operation Provide Comfort">Operation Provide Comfort</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> however, it left out Sulaymaniyah, Kirkuk and other important Kurdish populated regions. Bloody clashes between Iraqi forces and Kurdish troops continued and, after an uneasy and shaky balance of power was reached, the Iraqi government fully withdrew its military and other personnel from the region in October 1991 allowing Iraqi Kurdistan to function de facto independently. The region was to be ruled by the two principal Kurdish parties; the Kurdish Democratic Party (KDP) and the <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Union_of_Kurdistan" title="Patriotic Union of Kurdistan">Patriotic Union of Kurdistan</a> (PUK). The region also has its own flag and <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a>.
</p><p>At the same time, Iraq imposed an economic blockade over the region, reducing its oil and food supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/1992_Iraqi_Kurdistan_parliamentary_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1992 Iraqi Kurdistan parliamentary election">Elections held in June 1992</a> produced an inconclusive outcome, with the assembly divided almost equally between the two main parties and their allies. During this period, the Kurds were subjected to a double <a href="/wiki/Embargo" class="mw-redirect" title="Embargo">embargo</a>: one imposed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> on Iraq and one imposed by Saddam Hussein on their region. The severe economic hardships caused by the embargoes fueled tensions between the two dominant political parties, the KDP and the PUK, over control of trade routes and resources.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Relations between the PUK and the KDP started to become dangerously strained from September 1993 after rounds of amalgamations occurred between parties.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> This led to internecine and intra-Kurdish conflict and warfare between 1994 and 1996.
</p><p>After 1996, 13% of the Iraqi oil sales were allocated for Iraqi Kurdistan and this led to relative prosperity in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Saddam had established an oil smuggling route through territory controlled by the KDP, with the active involvement of senior Barzani family members. The taxation of this trade at the crossing point between Saddam’s territory and Kurdish controlled territory and then into Turkey, along with associated service revenue, meant that whoever controlled Dohuk and <a href="/wiki/Zakho" title="Zakho">Zakho</a> had the potential to earn several million dollars a week.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Direct <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> mediation led the two parties to a formal ceasefire in what was termed the <i>Washington Agreement</i> in September 1998. It is also argued that the <a href="/wiki/Oil-for-Food_Programme" title="Oil-for-Food Programme">Oil-for-Food Programme</a> from 1997 onward had an important effect on cessation of hostilities.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KurdishFederation1998.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/KurdishFederation1998.png/220px-KurdishFederation1998.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/KurdishFederation1998.png/330px-KurdishFederation1998.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/KurdishFederation1998.png/440px-KurdishFederation1998.png 2x" data-file-width="966" data-file-height="846" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KurdishFederation1998.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kurdish Federation in 1998</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="During_and_after_US-led_invasion">During and after US-led invasion</span></h4>
<p>Iraqi Kurds played an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>. Kurdish parties joined forces against the Iraqi government during the war in Spring 2003. Kurdish military forces, known as <a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Peshmerga</a>, played an important role in the overthrow of the Iraqi government;<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> however, Kurds have been reluctant to send troops into Baghdad since then, preferring not to be dragged into the sectarian struggle that so dominates much of Iraq.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Iraqi Kurds may be seen in two ways. The first and the most common way is to view the Kurds as victims, both of the central government in Iraq and of neighbouring powers – particularly Turkey. The second opposing position is to see them as an agent provocateur, acting as proxy forces for states opposed to the incumbent Iraqi regime.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A new constitution of Iraq was established in 2005, defining Iraq as a federalist state consisting of Regions and Governorates. It recognized both the Kurdistan Region and all laws passed by the KRG since 1992. There is provision for Governorates to create, join or leave Regions. However, as of late 2015, no new Regions have been formed, and the KRG remains the only regional government within Iraq.
</p><p>PUK leader <a href="/wiki/Jalal_Talabani" title="Jalal Talabani">Jalal Talabani</a> was elected President of the new Iraqi administration, while KDP leader <a href="/wiki/Masoud_Barzani" title="Masoud Barzani">Masoud Barzani</a> became President of the Kurdistan Regional Government.
</p><p>Since the downfall of the regime of Saddam Hussein, the relations between the KRG and Turkey have been in flux. Tensions marked a high stage in late February 2008 when <a href="/wiki/2008_Turkish_incursion_into_northern_Iraq" title="2008 Turkish incursion into northern Iraq">Turkey unilaterally took military action</a> against the PKK which at times uses the northern Iraq region as a base for militant activities against Turkey. The incursion, which lasted eight days, could have drawn the armed forces of Kurdistan into a broader regional war. However, relations have been improved since then, and Turkey now has the largest share of foreign investment in Kurdistan.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Following_US_withdrawal">Following US withdrawal</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Disputed_territories_of_Northern_Iraq" title="Disputed territories of Northern Iraq">Disputed territories of Northern Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan_independence_referendum,_2017" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum, 2017">Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum, 2017</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg/220px-Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg/330px-Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg/440px-Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Disputed_areas_in_Iraq.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Disputed areas in Iraq prior to the 2014 Northern Iraq offensive <div style="font-size: 85%;"><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff80e5; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Disputed and part of the Kurdish Regional Government since 1991.</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #decd87; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Disputed and under the control of central government.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Tensions between Iraqi Kurdistan and the central Iraqi government mounted through 2011–2012 on the issues of power sharing, oil production and territorial control. In April 2012, the president of Iraq's semi-autonomous northern Kurdish region demanded that officials agree to their demands or face the prospect of secession from Baghdad by September 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2012, the Iraqi government ordered the KRG to transfer its powers over the Peshmerga to the central government. Relations became further strained by the formation of a new command center (Tigris Operation Command) for Iraqi forces to operate in a disputed area over which both Baghdad and the <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Regional_Government" title="Kurdistan Regional Government">Kurdistan Regional Government</a> claim jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters1711_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1711-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> On 16 November 2012 a military clash between the Iraqi forces and the Peshmerga resulted in one person killed.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters1711_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters1711-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> CNN reported that two people were killed (one of them an Iraqi soldier) and ten wounded in clashes at the Tuz Khurmato town.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2014, Iraqi Kurdistan is in dispute with the Federal Iraqi government on the issues of territorial control, export of oil and budget distribution and is functioning largely outside Baghdad's control. With the escalation of the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_insurgency_(post-U.S._withdrawal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi insurgency (post-U.S. withdrawal)">Iraqi crisis</a> and fears of Iraq's collapse, Kurds have increasingly debated the issue of independence. During the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Iraq_offensive_(June_2014)" title="Northern Iraq offensive (June 2014)">2014 Northern Iraq offensive</a>, Iraqi Kurdistan seized the city of <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk" title="Kirkuk">Kirkuk</a> and the surrounding area, as well as most of the disputed territories in Northern Iraq. On 1 July 2014, <a href="/wiki/Masoud_Barzani" title="Masoud Barzani">Masoud Barzani</a> announced that "Iraq's Kurds will hold an independence referendum within months."<sup id="cite_ref-Kurdish_Leader:_We_Will_Vote_For_Independence_Soon_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurdish_Leader:_We_Will_Vote_For_Independence_Soon-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> After previously opposing the independence for Iraqi Kurdistan, Turkey later gave signs that it could recognize an independent Kurdish state.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurdish_Leader:_We_Will_Vote_For_Independence_Soon_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurdish_Leader:_We_Will_Vote_For_Independence_Soon-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_tide_is_finally_turning_for_the_Kurds_-_Especially_in_Turkey_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_tide_is_finally_turning_for_the_Kurds_-_Especially_in_Turkey-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> On 11 July 2014, KRG forces seized control of the Bai Hassan and <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk_Field" title="Kirkuk Field">Kirkuk oilfields</a>, prompting a condemnation from Baghdad and a threat of "dire consequences" if the oilfields were not relinquished back to Iraq's control.<sup id="cite_ref-KurdsOilfields_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KurdsOilfields-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pre-referendum,_pro-Kurdistan,_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil,_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/220px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/330px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg/440px-Pre-referendum%2C_pro-Kurdistan%2C_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil%2C_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pre-referendum,_pro-Kurdistan,_pro-independence_rally_in_Erbil,_Kurdistan_Region_of_Iraq_25.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pro-independence rally in <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a> in September 2017</div></div></div>
<p>In September, Kurdish leaders decided to postpone the referendum so as to focus on the fight against ISIL.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In November, <a href="/wiki/Ed_Royce" title="Ed Royce">Ed Royce</a>, Chairman of the Foreign Affairs Committee of the United States House of Representatives, introduced legislation to arm the Kurds directly, rather than continue working through the local governments.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2014, the US began a <a href="/wiki/American-led_intervention_in_Iraq_(2014%E2%80%93present)" title="American-led intervention in Iraq (2014–present)">campaign of airstrikes</a> in Iraq, in part to protect Kurdish areas such as Erbil from the militants.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2016, Kurdish president Barzani stated once again that "Now the time is ripe for the people of Kurdistan to decide their future through a referendum", supporting an <a href="/wiki/2017_Iraqi_Kurdistan_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="2017 Iraqi Kurdistan independence referendum">independence referendum</a> and citing similar referenda in Scotland, Catalonia and Quebec.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> On March 23, Barzani officially declared that Iraqi Kurdistan will hold the referendum some time "before October" of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> On April 2, 2017, the two governing parties released a joint statement announcing they would form a joint committee to prepare for a referendum to be held on 25 September.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_and_mineral_resources">Petroleum and mineral resources</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kurdistan_Regional_Government" title="Kurdistan Regional Government">KRG</a>-controlled parts of Iraqi Kurdistan contain 4 billion barrels of proven oil reserves. However, the KRG has estimated that the region contains around 45 billion barrels (7.2<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>9</sup>&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of unproven oil resource.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Extraction of these reserves began in 2007.
</p><p>In November 2011, Exxon challenged the Iraqi central government's authority with the signing of oil and gas contracts for exploration rights to six parcels of land in Kurdistan, including one contract in the disputed territories, just east of the <a href="/wiki/Kirkuk_Field" title="Kirkuk Field">Kirkuk mega-field</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>  This act caused Baghdad to threaten to revoke Exxon's contract in its southern fields, most notably the West-Qurna Phase 1 project.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Exxon responded by announcing its intention to leave the West-Qurna project.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of July 2007, the Kurdish government solicited foreign companies to invest in 40 new oil sites, with the hope of increasing regional oil production over the following 5 years by a factor of five, to about 1 million barrels per day (160,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Gas and associated gas reserves are in excess of 100<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>12</sup>&#160;cu&#160;ft (2,800&#160;km<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Notable companies active in Kurdistan include <a href="/wiki/Exxon" title="Exxon">Exxon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Total_S.A." title="Total S.A.">Total</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevron_Corporation" title="Chevron Corporation">Chevron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talisman_Energy" title="Talisman Energy">Talisman Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNO_ASA" title="DNO ASA">DNO</a>, <a href="/wiki/MOL_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="MOL Group">MOL Group</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genel_Energy" title="Genel Energy">Genel Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunt_Oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunt Oil">Hunt Oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Keystone_Petroleum" title="Gulf Keystone Petroleum">Gulf Keystone Petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marathon_Oil" title="Marathon Oil">Marathon Oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Other mineral resources that exist in significant quantities in the region include <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> (which is used to produce <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>), <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. The world's largest deposit of rock sulfur is located just southwest of <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Erbil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2012, Turkey and the Kurdistan Regional Government signed an agreement by which Turkey will supply the KRG with refined petroleum products in exchange for crude oil. Crude deliveries are expected to occur on a regular basis.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure_and_transport">Infrastructure and transport</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h3>
<p>Due to the devastation of the campaigns of the Iraqi army under Saddam Hussein and other former Iraqi regimes, the Kurdistan Region's infrastructure was never able to modernize. After the 1991 safe haven was established, the Kurdistan Regional Government began projects to reconstruct the Kurdistan Region. Since then, of all the 4,500 villages that were destroyed by Saddam Husseins' regime, 65% have been reconstructed by the KRG.<sup id="cite_ref-krg.org_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-krg.org-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mobility">Mobility</span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Sulaimaniyah International Airport" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg/200px-44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg/300px-44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg/400px-44929_The_Sulayamaniyah_International_Airport_in_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1504" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Erbil International Airport" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG/200px-Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="68" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG/300px-Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG/400px-Erbil_International_Airport_terminal_building.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3937" data-file-height="1346" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Airports of Sulaimaniyah and Erbil</div></div></div></div>
<p>Iraqi Kurdistan can be reached by land and air. By land, Iraqi Kurdistan can be reached most easily by Turkey through the Habur Border Gate which is the only border gate between Iraqi Kurdistan and Turkey. This border gate can be reached by bus or taxi from airports in Turkey as close as the <a href="/wiki/Mardin_Airport" title="Mardin Airport">Mardin</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diyarbakir_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Diyarbakir Airport">Diyarbakir</a> airports, as well as from <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>. Iraqi Kurdistan has two border gates with Iran, the Haji Omaran border gate and the Bashmeg border gate near the city of <a href="/wiki/Sulaymaniyah" title="Sulaymaniyah">Sulaymaniyah</a>. Iraqi Kurdistan has also a border gate with Syria known as the <a href="/wiki/Faysh_Khabur" title="Faysh Khabur">Faysh Khabur</a> border gate.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> From within Iraq, the Kurdistan Region can be reached by land from multiple roads.
</p><p>Iraqi Kurdistan has opened its doors to the international world by opening two international airports. <a href="/wiki/Erbil_International_Airport" title="Erbil International Airport">Erbil International Airport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulaimaniyah_International_Airport" title="Sulaimaniyah International Airport">Sulaimaniyah International Airport</a>, which both operate flights to <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> destinations. The KRG spent millions of dollars on the airports to attract international carriers, and currently Turkish Airlines, Austrian Airlines, Lufthansa, Etihad, Royal Jordanian, Emirates, Gulf Air, Middle East Airlines, Atlas Jet, and Fly Dubai all service the Region. There are at least 2 military airfields in Iraqi Kurdistan.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Geography"></span><span id="Climate"></span>
</p>
<div class="floatright"><a href="/wiki/File:Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG" class="image"><img alt="Administrative divisions of Kurdistan.PNG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG/264px-Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG" decoding="async" width="264" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG/396px-Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG/528px-Administrative_divisions_of_Kurdistan.PNG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="400" /></a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Iraq" title="Kurds in Iraq">Kurds in Iraq</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish people</a></div>
<p>Due to the absence of a proper <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a>, the exact population and demographics of Iraqi Kurdistan are unknown, but the <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_Regional_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish Regional Government">Kurdish Regional Government</a> has recently started to publish more detailed figures. The population of the region is notoriously difficult to ascertain, as the Iraqi government has historically sought to minimize the importance of the Kurdish minority while Kurdish groups have had a tendency to exaggerate the numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-StansfieldJomo2003_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StansfieldJomo2003-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Based on available data, Iraqi Kurdistan has a young population with an estimated 36% of the population being under the age of 15.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> As of 2018, the population of Iraqi Kurdistan had reached 5.9 million people. The ethnic make-up of Iraqi Kurdistan is diverse, including a <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> majority and significant ethnic minorities of <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmens" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Turkmens">Turkmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Assyrians" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Assyrians">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Armenians" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Armenians">Armenians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethno-religious" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethno-religious">ethno-religious</a> minorities of <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabak people">Shabaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarsan" class="mw-redirect" title="Yarsan">Kakai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidis</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<p>Kurdish is the most widely spoken language. The two main dialects of Kurdish are <a href="/wiki/Sorani" title="Sorani">Sorani</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kurmanji" title="Kurmanji">Kurmanji</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Kurmanji_Kurdish" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurmanji Kurdish">Bahdini</a> variant, but a part of the population also speaks <a href="/wiki/Hawrami" class="mw-redirect" title="Hawrami">Hawrami</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Halabja" title="Halabja">Halabja</a> region.  In <a href="/wiki/Shingal" class="mw-redirect" title="Shingal">Shingal</a> (also called Jebel Sinjar), people speak a <a href="/wiki/Kurmanji" title="Kurmanji">Kurmanji</a> dialect known as Shengali.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">Assyrian Neo-Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Neo-Aramaic" title="Chaldean Neo-Aramaic">Chaldean Neo-Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Turkmens#Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Iraqi Turkmens">Turkmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a> are also spoken by their respective communities.<sup id="cite_ref-KRG_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KRG-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Kurdistan" title="Religion in Kurdistan">Religion in Kurdistan</a></div>
<p>Iraqi Kurdistan has a religiously diverse population. The dominant religion is <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, which is professed by the majority of Iraqi Kurdistan's inhabitants. These include Kurds, Iraqi Turkmen, and Arabs, belonging mostly to the <a href="/wiki/Shafi%27i" class="mw-redirect" title="Shafi&#39;i">Shafi'i</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Sunni" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> Islam. There is also a small number of <a href="/wiki/Shia" class="mw-redirect" title="Shia">Shia</a> <a href="/wiki/Feyli_Kurds" class="mw-redirect" title="Feyli Kurds">Feyli Kurds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-masara_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masara-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> as well as adherents of <a href="/wiki/Sufi_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi Islam">Sufi Islam</a>.
</p><p>In 2015, the Kurdistan Regional Government enacted a law to formally protect religious minorities. <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Iraq" title="Christianity in Iraq">Christianity</a> is professed by <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrians in Iraq">Assyrians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenians_in_Iraq" title="Armenians in Iraq">Armenians</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Yezidism" class="mw-redirect" title="Yezidism">Yezidis</a> make up a significant minority, with some 650,000 in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> or 560,000 as of 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-masara_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masara-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Shabak_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Shabak people">Shabaki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yarsan" class="mw-redirect" title="Yarsan">Yarsan</a> (Ahl-e Haqq or Kakai) religions number around and 250,000 and 200,000 adherents respectively;<sup id="cite_ref-masara_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-masara-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> these, like Yezidism, are sometimes said to be related to the <a href="/wiki/Yazd%C3%A2nism" title="Yazdânism">pre-Islamic indigenous religion</a> of Kurdistan.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> religion have around 500 followers according to an official research from the religious affairs committee from the parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Zoroastrians were seeking official recognition of their religion as of early 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The first Zoroastrian temple was opened in the city of <a href="/wiki/Sulaymaniyah" title="Sulaymaniyah">Sulaymaniyah</a> (Silêmanî) in September 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A tiny ethno-religious community of <a href="/wiki/Mandeans" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandeans">Mandeans</a> also exists within the semi-autonomous region. <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish Jews">Kurdish Jews</a> number some several hundred families in the autonomous region.<sup id="cite_ref-JPost_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JPost-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
